        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/adma.202304091')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="169c8857ced1491f839b80e6420d542f"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="169c8857ced1491f839b80e6420d542f">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'b8c4089f-b934-430d-bd1c-eff07f5dc8db'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

        (function() {
            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {
                var store_domain = 'https://advanced.onlinelibrary.wiley.com';
                var session_id = '687D87B25D4A05ACCB22FAA25CC5798B';
                var url = ('https:' == document.location.protocol ? 'https://' : 'http://')
                    + "beacon.riskified.com?shop=" + store_domain + "&sid=" + session_id;
                var s = document.createElement('script');
                s.type = 'text/javascript';
                s.async = true;
                s.src = url;
                var x = document.getElementsByTagName('script')[0];
                x.parentNode.insertBefore(s, x);
            }
            if (window.attachEvent)
                window.attachEvent('onload', riskifiedBeaconLoad)
            else
                window.addEventListener('load', riskifiedBeaconLoad, false);
        })();

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d154188697" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Emerging sodium-ion batteries (NIBs) and potassium-ion batteries (KIBs) show promise in complementing lithium-ion battery (LIB) technology and diversifying the battery market. Hard carbon is a potential anode candidate for LIBs, NIBs, and KIBs due to its high capacity, sustainability, wide availability, and stable physicochemical properties. Herein, a series of hard carbons is synthesized by hydrothermal carbonization and subsequent pyrolysis at different temperatures to finely tune their structural properties. When tested as anodes, the hard carbons exhibit differing ion-storage trends for Li, Na, and K, with NIBs achieving the highest reversible capacity. Extensive materials and electrochemical characterizations are carried out to study the correlation of structural features with electrochemical performance and to explain the specific mechanisms of alkali-ion storage in hard carbons. In addition, the best-performing hard carbon is tested against a sodium cathode Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> in a Na-ion pouch cell, displaying a high power density of 2172&nbsp;W kg<sup>−1</sup> at an energy density of 181.5&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> (based on the total weight of active materials in both anode and cathode). The Na-ion pouch cell also shows stable ultralong-term cycling (9000 h or 5142 cycles) and demonstrates the promising potential of such materials as sustainable, scalable anodes for beyond Li-batteries.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202304091-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202304091-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The rapidly growing demand for lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) has already raised concerns over the scarcity of lithium and related components, leaving the supply chain for LIBs vulnerable unless more sustainable alternatives are sought out. Graphite has dominated as the anode since Sony commercialized its first-generation rechargeable LIBs in the late 20th century.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304091-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304091-bib-0001_R_d154188689e539" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Chem. Rec.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>For more sustainable post-Li technologies, sodium-ion (NIBs) and potassium-ion batteries (KIBs) using hard carbon anodes instead of graphite, are promising post-Li technologies,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304091-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304091-bib-0002_R_d154188689e546" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> sharing the analogous “rocking-chair” operation principle. Hard carbons demonstrate high capacity, structural stability, sustainability, and abundance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304091-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304091-bib-0003_R_d154188689e551" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Sustainable Syst. Adv. Energy Mater. Philos. Trans. R. Soc., A">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Indeed, hard carbons are used as high-power anodes in LIBs and are regarded as current market leaders for NIB anodes. In 2021, HiNa Battery Technology Co., Ltd launched the first Na-ion large-scale energy storage (1&nbsp;MWh) in China, while in the same year, CATL introduced its first-generation NIB using a hard carbon anode with an energy density of 160&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> for electric vehicles.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304091-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304091-bib-0004_R_d154188689e559" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Nano Today">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For KIBs, hard carbon anodes have shown surging popularity in recent years.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304091-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304091-bib-0005_R_d154188689e564" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Sci. Adv. InfoMat">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is believed that hard carbons will continue to play a significant role in alkali-ion large-scale and electric vehicle energy storage systems.</p>
         
         <p>Despite these advantages, the main shortcomings of hard carbon anodes in alkali-ion batteries arise from: 1) the low Coulombic efficiency (less than 80%) compared with over 92% for graphite in LIBs and 2) cycling underperformance compared with graphite anodes in LIBs. To optimize these materials for ion storage, it is critical to understand the mechanism of alkali-ion insertion into hard carbons. Several mechanisms have been proposed to account for the characteristic voltage–capacity profiles of the different alkali ions. It is reported that the&nbsp;“adsorption–intercalation” model has become one of the most representative storage mechanisms for Li<sup>+</sup> in hard carbons.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304091-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304091-bib-0006_R_d154188689e574" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Specifically, Li<sup>+</sup> adsorption occurs in the high-potential sloping region, and intercalation takes place during the low-potential plateau region. The corresponding DFT calculations have confirmed that the energetically favorable sites for adsorption include defects, edges, and microporous surfaces.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304091-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304091-bib-0006_R_d154188689e581" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For sodiation, in contrast, the low-potential plateau region is attributed to pore filling with metallic clusters forming, while in the high-voltage sloping region, Na<sup>+</sup> ions are preferentially stored in defects, pore surfaces, and within interlayers.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304091-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304091-bib-0007_R_d154188689e589" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Energy Mater. Nano Energy Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Energy Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The storage mechanism for K<sup>+</sup> within hard carbons is dominated by surface-driven pseudocapacitive processes with graphitic layer intercalation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304091-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304091-bib-0007_R_d154188689e597" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Energy Mater. Nano Energy Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Energy Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Such pseudocapacitive behavior is crucial to enhance both rate capability and cycle performance. Despite many fundamental studies on alkali-ion storage in hard carbons, it is important to note that hard carbons encompass a large family of materials with different surface areas, degrees of graphitization, porosity, and surface functionalities. Small variations in those features may lead to different ion-storage behaviors; hence, studying hard carbons with different structural features offers a more comprehensive understanding of the correlation between material structure and performance.</p>
         
         <p>It is of further interest to study the same material across different battery systems to be able to provide a full perspective of post-Li technologies. However, few reports investigating hard carbons as anodes in LIBs, NIBs, and KIBs in parallel exist to date.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304091-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304091-bib-0007_R_d154188689e605" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Energy Mater. Nano Energy Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Energy Mater.">7</a>, <a href="#adma202304091-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304091-bib-0008_R_d154188689e605" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Energy Mater. Chem. Mater. J. Mater. Chem. A Adv. Energy Mater. J. Mater. Chem. A">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In this work, a series of sustainable carbons with varied structural features was employed as anode in LIBs, NIBs, and KIBs, and the ion-storage behavior and electrochemical kinetics were characterized and compared to provide a broad insight into the effect of structure on energy storage ability. The hard carbons were synthesized via a facile hydrothermal carbonization process with a subsequent pyrolysis step ranging between 1000 °C and 1900 °C. By comparing their electrochemical performance in half cells and full cells, the material produced at 1500 °C in NIB was selected as the most promising candidate. To demonstrate the feasibility of this candidate in commercial batteries, Li-ion, Na-ion, and K-ion full cells were assembled, while a Na-ion pouch cell further demonstrated the scale-up potential and use in more realistic scenarios.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202304091-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202304091-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202304091-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202304091-sec-0030-title">2.1 Synthesis and Structural Characterization of Hard Carbons</h3>
            
            <p>In this work, hard carbons were obtained by hydrothermal carbonization (HTC) of a D-glucose solution at 230 °C for 12 h. The obtained carbon (HTC glucose) was then pyrolyzed at five different temperatures of 1000 °C, 1300 °C, 1500 °C, 1700 °C, and 1900 °C,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304091-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304091-bib-0009_R_d154188689e624" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Energy Environ. Sci.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> yielding the samples G1000, G1300, G1500, G1700, and G1900, respectively. The high-temperature treatment is a simple method to tune the surface chemistry and electronic properties of the HTC-derived hard carbon.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304091-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304091-bib-0009_R_d154188689e629" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Energy Environ. Sci.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images of the carbons obtained from HTC and pyrolysis showed minimal differences in morphology among hard carbon samples, with spherical particles of average size between 0.5 and 2.0&nbsp;µm (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202304091-fig-0001">1a</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). The typical spherical shape yielded from hydrothermal carbonization was beneficial for uniform electrochemical processes within an electrode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304091-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304091-bib-0003_R_d154188689e645" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Sustainable Syst. Adv. Energy Mater. Philos. Trans. R. Soc., A">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A disordered nanostructure was observed by transmission electron microscopy (TEM) in all hard carbon samples featuring curved, short-range graphitic areas (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304091-fig-0001">1a</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). However, upon closer examination of the TEM images, G1000 exhibited the most amorphous carbon structure, characterized by short and randomly stacked graphic regions. As the carbonization temperature increased, the carbon structure became more ordered, featuring long-ranged, flatter graphitic layers and pores, particularly evident in G1900.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202304091-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b0d71acf-5569-4f04-b72f-8142af29509d/adma202304091-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/b0d71acf-5569-4f04-b72f-8142af29509d/adma202304091-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/17ced0ce-cd77-451a-a48f-4f3d40b774b4/adma202304091-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b0d71acf-5569-4f04-b72f-8142af29509d/adma202304091-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202304091-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202304091" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Structural characterization and analysis for hard carbons. a) TEM of G1500 revealing curved, short-range graphitic areas. The insert shows the SEM image of the same material. b) WANS patterns. c) Raman spectra exhibiting the typical hard carbon features of the D band, G band, and 2D band. d) Normalized PDF showing interatomic distances in the hard carbons. e) Schematic diagram of the in-plane distances between carbon atoms labeled in the PDF. f) XPS survey of the hard carbon materials.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Wide-angle neutron scattering (WANS) and Raman spectroscopy were employed to investigate the graphitic nature and defect concentration of the carbon materials. The graphitic interlayer distance (<b>Table</b> <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#adma202304091-tbl-0001">1</a>, <i>d</i><sub>002</sub>) and number of layers in each graphitic domain were calculated from the (002) peak in the WANS (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304091-fig-0001">1b</a>), which varied according to pyrolysis temperature. With an increase in temperature, the position of the (002) peak shifted to a higher degree, with an accompanying decrease in full width at half maximum (FWHM). Correspondingly, G1000 had the widest average interlayer distance (4.10 Å) decreasing to 3.55 Å in G1900, while the number of layers in each graphitic domain increased from 3.02 in G1000 to 5.04 in G1900 (see Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information for more structural features). Besides <i>d</i>-spacing, an empirical <i>R</i>-value (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information, R), which refers to the ratio of the intensity of the (002) peak to the background, helps estimate the fraction of single graphitic layers in hard carbons.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304091-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304091-bib-0010_R_d154188689e701" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Carbon J. Power Sources">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The increase in <i>R</i>-value with pyrolysis temperature indicates the fraction of single layers in hard carbon decreasing with the pyrolysis temperature. In the Raman spectra of the hard carbons (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304091-fig-0001">1c</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information), the appearance and sharpening of the 2D band (≈2700 cm<sup>−1</sup>) with temperature was consistent with WANS data and indicated that increasing the pyrolysis temperature yielded a narrower interlayer distance and larger graphitic stacked domains.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304091-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304091-bib-0011_R_d154188689e717" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - ACS Energy Lett.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As well as qualitatively corroborating findings on layer stacking, Raman spectroscopy can probe defect concentration in carbon materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304091-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304091-bib-0012_R_d154188689e723" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Energy Environ. Sci.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The spectra of the hard carbons all exhibited a G band at ≈1580 cm<sup>−1</sup>, from in-plane sp<sup>2</sup> vibrations in rings or chains, and a D band at ≈1350 cm<sup>−1</sup>, which is characteristic of disorder induced by sp<sup>3</sup> hybridizations, such as the presence of defects and discontinuities in crystallites.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304091-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304091-bib-0013_R_d154188689e737" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Solid State Commun.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The peaks were broad and overlapping, and as the pyrolysis temperature increased, the intensity ratio of the D to G band (<i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub>, Table&nbsp;<a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#adma202304091-tbl-0001">1</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting information) increased, consistent with decreasing defect concentration typical of disordered hard carbons.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304091-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304091-bib-0014_R_d154188689e755" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Phys. Rev. B ACS Appl. Energy Mater. Carbon">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The local atomic structure and defect concentration<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304091-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304091-bib-0015_R_d154188689e760" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Prog. Energy">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304091-fig-0001">1d</a>) were also studied by pair distribution function (PDF) analysis of the neutron scattering data; the peaks labelled d<sub>1</sub> to d<sub>6</sub> correspond to the nearest distances between any two carbon atoms in a graphitic lattice (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304091-fig-0001">1e</a>). The strong signals for d<sub>1</sub> (≈1.4 Å), d<sub>2</sub> (≈2.5 Å), and d<sub>3</sub> (≈2.8 Å) confirm the presence of aromatic domains within the hard carbon samples. Further, from G1000 to G1900, the peaks d<sub>4</sub>, d<sub>5</sub>, and d<sub>6</sub> increased and sharpened, implying that pyrolysis induces greater long-range order within the in-plane carbon structure.</p>

               <div class="article-section__table-footnotes">
                  <ul>
                     <li id="adma202304091-tbl1-note-0001" class="footNotePopup__item" title="Footnote 1"><span class="number"><sup>a)</sup> </span>Calculated average interlayer distance by Bragg equation;</li>
                     <li id="adma202304091-tbl1-note-0002" class="footNotePopup__item" title="Footnote 2"><span class="number"><sup>b)</sup> </span>Fitted full width at half maximum of (002) peaks from WANS based on Gaussian models;</li>
                     <li id="adma202304091-tbl1-note-0003" class="footNotePopup__item" title="Footnote 3"><span class="number"><sup>c)</sup> </span>The number of layers in each graphitic stack (<i>c</i>-axis), from XRD;</li>

                     <li id="adma202304091-tbl1-note-0005" class="footNotePopup__item" title="Footnote 5"><span class="number"><sup>e)</sup> </span>Calculated using the Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) method for N<sub>2</sub> adsorption;</li>
