                  </ul>
               </div>
               <div class="article-section__table-source"></div>
            </div>
            
            <p>To investigate the degree of open and closed porosity formed during HTC and pyrolysis, both gas adsorption/desorption and small angle neutron scattering (SANS) were performed. The calculated specific surface areas derived from adsorption/desorption isotherms with different probe molecule sizes (N<sub>2</sub> and CO<sub>2</sub>) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information) showed a negative correlation between pyrolysis temperature and accessible surface area (Table&nbsp;<a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#adma202304091-tbl-0001">1</a>). It is important to note that the BET analysis primarily provided information on the open pore structure and size distribution, rather than the porosity of closed pores in hard carbon.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304091-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304091-bib-0016_R_d154188689e1084" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Funct. Mater. ACS Energy Lett.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This open surface area represented the accessible surface of the active material, which allowed the potential penetration by the electrolyte. However, a higher surface area that was accessible to the electrolyte could result in increased electrolyte decomposition, leading to the formation of a solid electrolyte interphase (SEI). This SEI formation could contribute to irreversible cation loss, irreversible capacity loss; and subsequently, a lower Coulombic efficiency.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304091-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304091-bib-0017_R_d154188689e1090" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Energy Storage Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The carbon materials carbonized at 1000 °C exhibited a surface area of 382 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, which led to the excessive formation of solid electrolyte interphase. These structural properties aligned well with the lower initial Coulombic efficiencies (ICE) observed in G1000 in LIBs, NIBs, and KIBs (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). Meanwhile, average pore diameters (Table&nbsp;<a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#adma202304091-tbl-0001">1, D</a>) estimated from SANS<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304091-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304091-bib-0018_R_d154188689e1106" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Electrochem. Soc. J. Electrochem. Soc.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information) increased with the pyrolysis temperature, from 1.39&nbsp;nm (G1000) to 2.29&nbsp;nm (G1500) to 4.93&nbsp;nm (G1900). Together, these results imply that the higher-temperature samples are dominated by closed porosity, which is known to be important for Na-ion storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304091-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304091-bib-0016_R_d154188689e1118" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Funct. Mater. ACS Energy Lett.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides porosity, surface groups play a critical role in reversible and irreversible ion storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304091-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304091-bib-0019_R_d154188689e1123" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Carbon">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304091-fig-0001">1f</a>) was employed to identify the surface species of HTC glucose and hard carbon samples. Quantification from fitted core-level XPS spectra for C1s and O1s (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information) shows that as the pyrolysis temperature increases, the amount of oxygen (C─O) decreases, from 5.9 at% to 1.3 at% (Table&nbsp;<a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#adma202304091-tbl-0001">1</a>).</p>
            
            <p>Overall, these five hard carbon samples are ideal for a systematic comparison of alkali-ion storage because: 1) the closed porosity pore, with sizes ranging from 1.0 to 5.0&nbsp;nm, is ideal for Na-ion pore-filling;<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304091-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304091-bib-0020_R_d154188689e1141" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. J. Am. Chem. Soc.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> 2) the average <i>d</i>-spacings are all within 4.1 Å yet larger than that of graphite (3.35 Å), allowing higher ion diffusion and storage; and 3) G1000 with high surface area is useful for studying the irreversible loss during the first cycle, while the other carbons have a low surface area of ≈10 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, ideal for suppressing excessive SEI formation.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202304091-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202304091-sec-0040-title">2.2 Galvanostatic Performance and Rate Capability in LIBs, NIBs, and KIBs</h3>
            
            <p>To understand the effect of hard carbon structure on electrochemical performance, Li-, Na-, and K-ion half cells were first assembled in parallel with the corresponding electrolyte system (Experimental Section in the Supporting Information). Rate capability (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202304091-fig-0002">2a,d,g</a>) was investigated between 0.1 and 2 C (1 C = 300&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> for LIBs and NIBs, and 1 C = 250&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> for KIBs), and analysis of the galvanostatic discharge–charge profile (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304091-fig-0002">2b,e,h</a>) was carried out at 0.1 C in the second cycle to consider only reversible capacity (see Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6–S9</a>, Supporting Information for initial Coulombic efficiency and first five discharge–charge profiles).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202304091-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/7ef377fa-bfa4-48bd-a350-b3f121de66ea/adma202304091-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/7ef377fa-bfa4-48bd-a350-b3f121de66ea/adma202304091-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2fad5e5a-7257-49d5-8011-76b774e1adad/adma202304091-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/7ef377fa-bfa4-48bd-a350-b3f121de66ea/adma202304091-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202304091-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202304091" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical characterization (half-cell) for hard carbons used as anodes in LIBs, NIBs, and KIBs. a,d,g) Rate capability of the hard carbon anodes at different current densities of LIBs (a), NIBs (d), and KIBs (g). b,e,h) Galvanostatic discharge–charge profile of the 2nd cycle in LIBs at 30&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> (b),&nbsp;NIBs at 30&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> (e), and KIBs at 25&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> (h).&nbsp;c,f,i) Capacity contribution from two different potential ranges of LIBs (c), NIBs (f), and KIBs (i).</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>In LIBs, G1000 delivered the highest capacity at each current density (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304091-fig-0002">2a</a>) with more than 350 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 30&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> and maintaining 181 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> even at 600&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>. Interlayers with <i>d</i>-spacings ranging from 3–8 Å are theoretically shown to be energetically favorable sites for Li<sup>+[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304091-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304091-bib-0009_R_d154188689e1221" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Energy Environ. Sci.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> adsorption. As the disordered structure of hard carbons gives electronically and geometrically nonequivalent sites, Li<sup>+</sup> adsorption occurs across the entire voltage range; and thus, the discharge profile in LIBs (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304091-fig-0002">2b</a>) presents a long sloping region with no obvious staging mechanism observed. The capacity contribution of LIBs from the high-potential region (&gt;0.1&nbsp;V vs Li<sup>+</sup>/Li) and the low-potential region (1–100&nbsp;mV) both decrease with the increase in pyrolysis temperature (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304091-fig-0002">2c</a>). The differential capacity plot<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304091-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304091-bib-0021_R_d154188689e1237" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Langmuir Battery Energy">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> of lithiation of hard carbon derived from the second discharge–charge cycle at a current density of 30&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10a</a>, Supporting Information) shows the distinct potential ranges. The high-potential capacity in LIBs is typically attributed to surface electrochemical reactions involving edge and defect sites and adsorption to the surface of pores.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304091-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304091-bib-0008_R_d154188689e1248" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Energy Mater. Chem. Mater. J. Mater. Chem. A Adv. Energy Mater. J. Mater. Chem. A">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The low-potential region is associated with the insertion of Li<sup>+</sup> into pseudo-graphitic layers.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304091-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304091-bib-0006_R_d154188689e1255" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Hence, G1000's highest Li<sup>+</sup> ion acceptance ability is attributed to the high degree of defects providing more sites for high-potential capacity and its largest <i>d</i>-spacing able to accommodate more Li-ions within the interlayers in the low-potential region. In addition, the high fraction of single graphene layers within G1000 (<i>R</i> = 2.0, Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information) can enhance Li-ion storage via surface adsorption.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304091-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304091-bib-0010_R_d154188689e1270" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Carbon J. Power Sources">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> On the other hand, G1900 only delivers ≈160 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> compared with over 350 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> in graphite. This could be because cross-linking between the pseudo-graphitic domains in G1900 prevents the graphene layers from expanding effectively to accommodate Li ions to form a concentrated stage 1 graphite intercalation compound, limiting Li-ion-storage ability.</p>
            
            <p>In contrast to the trends exhibited by hard carbons in LIBs, G1000 as a NIB anode outperformed the other carbons at higher current densities, while below 1C, G1500 achieved the highest capacities (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304091-fig-0002">2d</a>). Owing to the largest surface area and defective structure, G1000 delivered the highest sloping capacity (&gt; ≈0.1&nbsp;V, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304091-fig-0002">2e,f</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information) with negligible plateau contribution, whereas the higher temperature materials presented capacity in both sloping and long plateau regions. At current densities above 300&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> where the polarization caused the cutoff voltage to be reached prematurely,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304091-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304091-bib-0022_R_d154188689e1294" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Chem. Commun.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> only sloping capacity was observed for all hard carbons; thus, G1000 retained the largest capacity. At lower currents; however, the capacity of G1000 was limited by the small pore size (1.39&nbsp;nm) restricting Na<sup>+</sup> pore-filling<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304091-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304091-bib-0009_R_d154188689e1302" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Energy Environ. Sci.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> that would occur at the low-potential plateau region. Instead, G1500 had the highest reversible capacity of over 340 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 30&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>. The difference in the plateau region during sodiation among hard carbon samples could be observed from differential capacity plots in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10b</a>, Supporting Information. The balance of defects, enlarged interlayers, and porosity enabled access to high capacity in both the sloping and plateau region. Interestingly, G1500 had a comparable sloping capacity to G1000 but maintained a high plateau capacity. More importantly, G1500 in NIBs had the highest ICE (86.90%) of all the carbons, comparable with ≈90% in graphite||Li cells. This high ICE was important in full cells because it reduced the amount of cathode needed to balance the loss of capacity in the anode, and in turn, enhanced the energy density. Hence, G1500 was a promising candidate for both a high-power and high-energy anode. Above 1500 °C, despite the larger pore sizes, the narrow interlayer distances limited the diffusion of Na ions toward the pores, resulting in low reversible capacities in G1700 and G1900.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304091-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304091-bib-0009_R_d154188689e1315" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Energy Environ. Sci.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>In KIBs, the hard carbons demonstrated the same trend as in LIBs: the higher the carbonization temperature, the lower the capacity (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304091-fig-0002">2g</a>) for all current densities. The discharge–charge profile (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304091-fig-0002">2h</a>) shows a clear sloping region (0.25–2.5&nbsp;V) and a semi-plateau region (1&nbsp;mV to 0.25&nbsp;V). Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10c</a>, Supporting Information shows the differential capacity plots evidencing the semi-plateau regions during potassiation of hard carbons. G1000 has the highest sloping capacity but the lowest semi-plateau capacity (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304091-fig-0002">2i</a>), in common with LIBs and NIBs. As the temperature increases, the semi-plateau capacity increases while the sloping capacity decreases. Adsorption of larger K ions within expanded graphitic layers (&gt;3.85 Å) has previously been shown to be energetically favorable;<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304091-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304091-bib-0009_R_d154188689e1334" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Energy Environ. Sci.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and thus, occurs preferentially across higher voltages in G1000, which has an average interlayer distance of 4.10 Å. As the interlayers narrow with increasing temperature, the capacity contribution between 0.25 and 2.5&nbsp;V is negligible for G1700 and G1900, with K<sup>+</sup> intercalation behavior instead observed around 0.38&nbsp;V (matching that in graphite||K cells<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304091-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304091-bib-0023_R_d154188689e1342" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. J. Mater. Chem. A">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>). However, intercalation of K<sup>+</sup> is kinetically far slower than adsorption, which further reduces the specific capacity, especially at high current densities (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304091-fig-0002">2g</a>). Unlike adsorption and intercalation, pore-filling in hard carbon during potassiation is rarely reported. We performed ex situ small-angle X-ray scattering (SAXS) using G1500 to probe the changes in the electron density at different voltages (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information). This ex-situ method has been used by our group to investigate the pore filling in hard carbons for Na-ion storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304091-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304091-bib-0024_R_d154188689e1356" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Mater. Chem. A">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The fitted electronic densities Δρ showed negligible variation between the fully potassiated and de-potassiated states (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information), implying that pore-filling did not happen during potassium insertion. This phenomenon may be due to the slow diffusion rate of K<sup>+</sup> ions between the interlayers, preventing ions from reaching the pores.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304091-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304091-bib-0009_R_d154188689e1367" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Energy Environ. Sci.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For G1700 and G1900 in KIBs, both show semi-plateau regions during the insertion of K<sup>+</sup> ions and low ICEs (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10c</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). K<sup>+</sup> intercalation into graphite can expand the graphite from 0.335 to 0.535&nbsp;nm.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304091-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304091-bib-0023_R_d154188689e1383" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. J. Mater. Chem. A">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Assuming the final expansion of the <i>d</i>-spacing remains constant, the more graphitic regions (with narrower <i>d</i>-spacing) will need to undergo a greater expansion compared to the more disordered carbon (with wider <i>d</i>-spacing). Greater expansion means more SEI formation; thus, the more graphitic carbon exhibits a lower ICE.</p>
            
            <p>It is interesting to note that even though Li<sup>+</sup> and K<sup>+</sup> intercalation into short-ranged graphitic layers is still applicable to hard carbons,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304091-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304091-bib-0008_R_d154188689e1402" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Energy Mater. Chem. Mater. J. Mater. Chem. A Adv. Energy Mater. J. Mater. Chem. A">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the ion capacities achieved by both G1700 and G1900 fall short of graphite intercalation compounds LiC<sub>6</sub> and KC<sub>8.</sub><sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304091-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304091-bib-0025_R_d154188689e1411" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Instead, the most promising hard carbon anode for LIBs and KIBs is G1000, where fast ion adsorption is dominant; whereas G1500 demonstrates the highest capacity for NIBs, where Na<sup>+</sup> storage is crucial.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202304091-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202304091-sec-0050-title">2.3 Electrochemical Kinetics</h3>
            
            <p>To further study the ion-storage kinetics, cyclic voltammetry at varying scan rates of 0.1 to 2&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> was performed (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12–S14</a>, Supporting Information). The capacity of hard carbon anodes contains both surface-controlled (adsorption at, for e.g., defect sites, edges, and micropore surface) and diffusion-controlled (ion diffusion into graphitic layers or pore filling) contributions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304091-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304091-bib-0008_R_d154188689e1432" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Energy Mater. Chem. Mater. J. Mater. Chem. A Adv. Energy Mater. J. Mater. Chem. A">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The relationship between the response current (<i>i</i>) and scan rate (<i>v</i>) is given by <i>i</i> = <i>av<sup>b</sup></i> (where <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> are both adjustable constants).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304091-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304091-bib-0026_R_d154188689e1453" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Chem. Educ.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For a given potential, <i>a</i> × <i>b</i> ≈ 1 denotes a more pseudocapacitive-like behavior while <i>a</i> × <i>b</i> ≈ 0.5 indicates the reactions are mainly controlled by semi-infinite diffusion of ions in battery-type processes. The summarized <i>b</i> values at 0.4&nbsp;V and 1&nbsp;mV from cathodic scans in LIBs, NIBs, and KIBs are shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202304091-fig-0003">3a–c</a>, while the fittings are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information. 0.4&nbsp;V represents the sloping region while 1&nbsp;mV is the low-potential plateau (or semi-plateau) region.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202304091-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/83e7dafe-43dc-4246-9326-598db20520fd/adma202304091-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/83e7dafe-43dc-4246-9326-598db20520fd/adma202304091-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1912369f-0cd7-4a3e-8c73-375215ed161b/adma202304091-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/83e7dafe-43dc-4246-9326-598db20520fd/adma202304091-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202304091-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202304091" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical kinetics of Li<sup>+</sup>, Na<sup>+</sup>, and K<sup>+</sup> in hard carbons during lithiation, sodiation, and potassiation. a–c) Calculated <i>b</i> values of hard carbon samples at 0.4&nbsp;V and 1&nbsp;mV in LIBs (a), NIBs (b), and KIBs (c). d–f) The percentage of surface-controlled contribution at different scan rates of all hard carbons in LIBs (d), NIBs (e), and KIBs (f).</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>In LIBs, for all the carbons upon discharging, the <i>b</i> values are close to 1 at 0.4&nbsp;V (V vs Li<sup>+</sup>/Li) and nearer to 0.5 at 1&nbsp;mV (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304091-fig-0003">3a</a>). These values corroborate our earlier observations of fast, pseudo-capacitive Li<sup>+</sup> adsorption occurring at defects, edges, and pore surfaces at high voltages and diffusion-controlled insertion into pseudo-graphitic layers at lower voltages, in all materials. In the case of NIBs, <i>b</i> values of G1000, G1300, and G1500 at 0.4&nbsp;V (V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) are close to 1 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304091-fig-0003">3b</a>), becoming closer to 0.5 at lower potential. These results once again confirm our previous hypothesis that Na<sup>+</sup> storage in the sloping region is largely pseudo-capacitive, while pore filling is the predominant storage phenomenon in the plateau region. In contrast, <i>b</i> values of G1700 and G1900 are close to 0.5 at both high and low potentials because there are fewer defect and edge sites for adsorption, resulting in pore filling as the main storage process. In KIBs, the <i>b</i> values vary from close to 1 down to 0.5, with pyrolysis temperature (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304091-fig-0003">3c</a>). This trend is largely consistent with the general shift from pseudocapacitive K<sup>+</sup> adsorption processes in G1000, with the widest interlayers and highest defect concentration, to diffusion-controlled intercalation dominating ion storage in G1700 and G1900, with more graphite-like discharge behavior. For G1300 and G1500, the difference in <i>b</i> values at high and low voltage supports the adsorption–intercalation model. Overall, the <i>b</i> value trends across LIBs, NIBs, and KIBs are consistent with the different storage affinities of each carbon with Li<sup>+</sup>, Na<sup>+</sup>, and K<sup>+</sup>. These differences are further highlighted by comparing how the contribution of surface-controlled processes varies with the scan rate between the samples (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304091-fig-0003">3d–f</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information). In LIBs, all samples show a similar increase in surface-controlled current with scan rate (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304091-fig-0003">3d</a>), indicating similar fast storage processes in all carbons. In contrast, for NIBs and KIBs (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304091-fig-0003">3e,f</a>), diffusion-controlled processes are important in G1700 and G1900 but are too slow to contribute to ion storage at higher scan rates, resulting in poor rate performance.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202304091-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202304091-sec-0060-title">2.4 Diffusion Coefficients</h3>
            
            <p>Through the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT), it is possible to calculate and analyze the diffusion coefficients (or diffusivity) of alkali-ions (<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/8c2d5c08-0e38-438f-bfc5-99b520a0b3e8/adma202304091-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202304091:adma202304091-math-0001" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202304091-math-0001.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <msub>
                        <mi>D</mi>
                        <msup>
                           <mi>Li</mi>
                           <mo>+</mo>
                        </msup>
                     </msub>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${D}_{{\mathrm{Li}}^ + }$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math>, <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/33a04596-ed78-406e-9f8f-fba1f2ef2a0e/adma202304091-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202304091:adma202304091-math-0002" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202304091-math-0002.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <msub>
                        <mi>D</mi>
                        <msup>
                           <mi>Na</mi>
                           <mo>+</mo>
                        </msup>
                     </msub>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${D}_{{\mathrm{Na}}^ + }$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math>, <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/a6f00408-7f31-4d41-b249-34b0ef0a99f2/adma202304091-math-0003.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202304091:adma202304091-math-0003" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202304091-math-0003.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <msub>
                        <mi>D</mi>
                        <msup>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal">K</mi>
                           <mo>+</mo>
                        </msup>
                     </msub>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${D}_{{{\mathrm{K}}}^ + }$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math>) at different potentials. In this work, GITT was performed from the 2nd cycle by discharging and charging at a pulsed current of 0.1 C for 30 min (30&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> for LIBs and NIBs, 25&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> for KIBs) followed by a rest of 3 h to allow the voltage to become stable, between 2.5 V and 1&nbsp;mV. The GITT profiles are shown in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17a, S18a</a>, and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20a</a>, Supporting Information, and the corresponding <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/e1e925fa-eec9-492c-bdc1-62ee683ddf68/adma202304091-math-0004.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202304091:adma202304091-math-0004" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202304091-math-0004.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <msub>
                        <mi>D</mi>
                        <msup>
                           <mi>Li</mi>
                           <mo>+</mo>
                        </msup>
                     </msub>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${D}_{{\mathrm{Li}}^ + }$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math>, <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/23581fe3-2be0-4efc-94b3-eda8abfb93bc/adma202304091-math-0005.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202304091:adma202304091-math-0005" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202304091-math-0005.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <msub>
                        <mi>D</mi>
                        <msup>
                           <mi>Na</mi>
                           <mo>+</mo>
                        </msup>
                     </msub>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${D}_{{\mathrm{Na}}^ + }$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math>, <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/95b9085b-06f1-45a4-876d-8a1e0dca2d8b/adma202304091-math-0006.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202304091:adma202304091-math-0006" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202304091-math-0006.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <msub>
                        <mi>D</mi>
                        <msup>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal">K</mi>
                           <mo>+</mo>
                        </msup>
                     </msub>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${D}_{{{\mathrm{K}}}^ + }$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math> are displayed in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202304091-fig-0004">4</a>.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202304091-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/758b40a7-12ca-463c-8dbd-81bb9fc1e493/adma202304091-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/758b40a7-12ca-463c-8dbd-81bb9fc1e493/adma202304091-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d70ef7e3-6c55-4166-bd6f-a3687bfc96a9/adma202304091-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/758b40a7-12ca-463c-8dbd-81bb9fc1e493/adma202304091-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202304091-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202304091" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Diffusion coefficients of Li<sup>+</sup>, Na<sup>+</sup>, and K<sup>+</sup> in hard carbons during lithiation, sodiation, and potassiation. a–c) Calculated diffusion coefficients of Li<sup>+</sup> during lithiation (a), Na<sup>+</sup> during sodiation (b), and K<sup>+</sup> during potassiation (c) in hard carbons. These diffusion coefficients were calculated from GITT in the second cycle to avoid the influence of SEI formation during the first cycle.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>In LIBs, during lithiation, <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/aaec3d3e-e654-4807-9303-17c81416dfc8/adma202304091-math-0007.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202304091:adma202304091-math-0007" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202304091-math-0007.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <msub>
                        <mi>D</mi>
                        <msup>
                           <mi>Li</mi>
                           <mo>+</mo>
                        </msup>
                     </msub>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${D}_{{\mathrm{Li}}^ + }$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304091-fig-0004">4a</a>) decreases gradually for all hard carbon samples. Initially, at high potential, Li<sup>+</sup> adsorption is energetically favored at defects such as edge and interlayer sites; once these sites are occupied, diffusion of lithium ions from these locations to less energetically favorable sites is slowed or subsequent incoming lithium ions are blocked, thus, slowing down the insertion process. This behavior corresponds well with a decrease in <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/bae3840c-53fe-48ac-8b11-bab2e03a390c/adma202304091-math-0008.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202304091:adma202304091-math-0008" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202304091-math-0008.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <msub>
                        <mi>D</mi>
                        <msup>
                           <mi>Li</mi>
                           <mo>+</mo>
                        </msup>
                     </msub>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${D}_{{\mathrm{Li}}^ + }$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math> between 0.3 V and 1&nbsp;mV, indicating that Li<sup>+</sup> adsorption is less kinetically favorable at lower voltages. During de-lithiation (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17b</a>, Supporting Information), the diffusion coefficients gradually decrease as the potential increases. Li<sup>+</sup> ions are released from low energy adsorption sites at low potential and travel out of the carbon structure; subsequent diffusion away from energetically favorable sites at high potential occurs more slowly, resulting in a drop in the diffusion coefficient.</p>
            
            <p>During sodiation, high <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/d9143241-a63c-44b0-a135-7f688706feab/adma202304091-math-0009.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202304091:adma202304091-math-0009" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202304091-math-0009.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <mrow>
                        <msub>
                           <mi>D</mi>
                           <msup>
                              <mi>Na</mi>
                              <mo>+</mo>
                           </msup>
                        </msub>
                        <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                     </mrow>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${D}_{{\mathrm{Na}}^ + }\ $</annotation>
                  </semantics></math>(Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304091-fig-0004">4b</a>) in the sloping region suggests that easily accessible sites offer kinetically fast adsorption until a sharp decrease between ≈0.1&nbsp;V and ≈0.05&nbsp;V. Such a sharp decrease in <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/4751248a-ec6e-4dfe-bfea-651ada6e9a09/adma202304091-math-0010.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202304091:adma202304091-math-0010" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202304091-math-0010.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <msub>
                        <mi>D</mi>
                        <msup>
                           <mi>Na</mi>
                           <mo>+</mo>
                        </msup>
                     </msub>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${D}_{{\mathrm{Na}}^ + }$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math> implies that after the energetically favorable sites are filled, diffusion pathways to the lower energy sites are hindered. Below ≈0.05&nbsp;V, <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/42ba51b7-aa0c-4564-9e5f-29807c8f19d9/adma202304091-math-0011.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202304091:adma202304091-math-0011" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202304091-math-0011.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <msub>
                        <mi>D</mi>
                        <msup>
                           <mi>Na</mi>
                           <mo>+</mo>
                        </msup>
                     </msub>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${D}_{{\mathrm{Na}}^ + }$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math> of G1300 and G1500 increase. Curved graphitic planes have been shown to lower ion migration barriers;<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304091-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304091-bib-0009_R_d154188689e1756" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Energy Environ. Sci.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> therefore, an increase in <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/4b88cb5f-59b8-4a04-9e7d-c6d23540ac01/adma202304091-math-0012.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202304091:adma202304091-math-0012" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202304091-math-0012.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <msub>
                        <mi>D</mi>
                        <msup>
                           <mi>Na</mi>
                           <mo>+</mo>
                        </msup>
                     </msub>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${D}_{{\mathrm{Na}}^ + }$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math> at these voltages is consistent with pore filling occurring in the plateau region.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304091-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304091-bib-0020_R_d154188689e1772" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. J. Am. Chem. Soc.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This increase is largely beneficial for fast-charging because if the Na-ion diffusion rate is lower than the migration rate, plating of metallic sodium can occur on the surface of the electrode. Therefore, a high <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/6cb77e4c-2859-4967-b84b-f6f875e6240f/adma202304091-math-0013.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202304091:adma202304091-math-0013" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202304091-math-0013.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <msub>
                        <mi>D</mi>
                        <msup>
                           <mi>Na</mi>
                           <mo>+</mo>
                        </msup>
                     </msub>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${D}_{{\mathrm{Na}}^ + }$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math> at the end of sodiation can help reduce the risk of plating. In contrast, <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/01c64b27-6b0f-4c0d-8c8f-dfb3c58085e3/adma202304091-math-0014.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202304091:adma202304091-math-0014" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202304091-math-0014.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <msub>
                        <mi>D</mi>
                        <msup>
                           <mi>Na</mi>
                           <mo>+</mo>
                        </msup>
                     </msub>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${D}_{{\mathrm{Na}}^ + }$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math> of G1000, G1700, and G1900 do not increase at the end of sodiation. The small pores within G1000 inhibit pore-filling while the narrower interlayer distances in G1700 and G1900 largely impede Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion into most pores; thus, detrimentally affecting plateau capacity. Further, GITT at a lower current (15&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>) is performed on G1900 (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a>, Supporting Information), showing the same <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/387f9798-38df-4e62-87b9-11797fec66c3/adma202304091-math-0015.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202304091:adma202304091-math-0015" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202304091-math-0015.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <msub>
                        <mi>D</mi>
                        <msup>
                           <mi>Na</mi>
                           <mo>+</mo>
                        </msup>
                     </msub>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${D}_{{\mathrm{Na}}^ + }$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math> trend as that in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304091-fig-0004">4b</a>. Even with more time to diffuse within the G1900 (lower polarisation), the speed of ion diffusion to pores does not increase. G1500 and G1300 with larger <i>d</i>-spacing and more defective structures provide higher <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/c3851c46-1af5-47b0-88d5-d81998791e50/adma202304091-math-0016.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202304091:adma202304091-math-0016" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202304091-math-0016.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <msub>
                        <mi>D</mi>
                        <msup>
                           <mi>Na</mi>
                           <mo>+</mo>
                        </msup>
                     </msub>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${D}_{{\mathrm{Na}}^ + }$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math> for pore filling. Hence, it is reasonable to speculate that both defect sites and interlayer distances play important roles in accelerating the process of Na-ion pore filling such as passing the pores. During de-sodiation (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18b</a>, Supporting Information), <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/77ded4d4-3386-48c6-bca3-dab96e0e8900/adma202304091-math-0017.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202304091:adma202304091-math-0017" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202304091-math-0017.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <msub>
                        <mi>D</mi>
                        <msup>
                           <mi>Na</mi>
                           <mo>+</mo>
                        </msup>
                     </msub>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${D}_{{\mathrm{Na}}^ + }$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math> decreases across all hard carbons between 1&nbsp;mV and 0.1&nbsp;V when Na<sup>+</sup> ions diffuse away from closed pores through graphitic layers. Above 0.1&nbsp;V, surface desorption of Na<sup>+</sup> occurs in an order of weakly bonded sites to strongly bonded sites. Thus, an “n” shape of <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/8e0913cc-b6c7-4645-96bd-c3b83044d1e5/adma202304091-math-0018.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202304091:adma202304091-math-0018" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202304091-math-0018.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <msub>
                        <mi>D</mi>
                        <msup>
                           <mi>Na</mi>
                           <mo>+</mo>
                        </msup>
                     </msub>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${D}_{{\mathrm{Na}}^ + }$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math> might be seen above 0.1&nbsp;V.</p>
            
            <p>During potassiation, <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/075c3eaa-0dd5-489e-a8ad-3ed5ebc74259/adma202304091-math-0019.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202304091:adma202304091-math-0019" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202304091-math-0019.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <msub>
                        <mi>D</mi>
                        <mrow>
                           <msup>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">K</mi>
                              <mo>+</mo>
                           </msup>
                           <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                        </mrow>
                     </msub>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${D}_{{{\mathrm{K}}}^ + \ }$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math> decreases steadily with decreasing potential (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304091-fig-0004">4c</a>). In contrast to <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/830cc01c-959d-41df-9161-cdb5ac4529f1/adma202304091-math-0020.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202304091:adma202304091-math-0020" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202304091-math-0020.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <msub>
                        <mi>D</mi>
                        <msup>
                           <mi>Li</mi>
                           <mo>+</mo>
                        </msup>
                     </msub>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${D}_{{\mathrm{Li}}^ + }$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math>, as successive K<sup>+</sup> ions are intercalated, they must overcome the energy barrier of repulsive forces from already intercalated K<sup>+</sup> and the van der Waals forces from the π–π graphite stacks, both of which slow the diffusion of ions. At the beginning of depotassiation, much higher <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/e2c3152d-41a6-405f-936a-a2376804dc29/adma202304091-math-0021.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202304091:adma202304091-math-0021" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202304091-math-0021.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <msub>
                        <mi>D</mi>
                        <mrow>
                           <msup>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">K</mi>
                              <mo>+</mo>
                           </msup>
                           <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                        </mrow>
                     </msub>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${D}_{{{\mathrm{K}}}^ + \ }$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math> values (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20b</a>, Supporting Information) imply very fast diffusion of K<sup>+</sup> ions extraction from hard carbons, which may be largely driven by the energetically favorable restoration of π–π graphitic stacking.</p>
            
            <p>Overall, despite having ion-storage capacities in a similar range, the hard carbons exhibit different storage mechanisms for Li, Na, and K. Li<sup>+</sup> storage in the hard carbons shows a greater reliance on surface-controlled adsorption processes, resulting in G1000, with the greatest availability of defects and surfaces as the best-performing material at all current densities. In the case of K<sup>+</sup>, a shift from adsorption to intercalation processes is observed as the carbon structure evolves with temperature; although, the highest capacity is still achieved in G1000 with the highest accessibility of adsorption sites. In NIBs, the structural features are more interdependent: pore size increases with temperature but access to these pores is reliant on the diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> through interlayers and is only possible at lower temperatures. G1500; therefore, combines the best compromise of defects, expanded interlayers, and open pores to achieve the highest capacity.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202304091-sec-0070">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202304091-sec-0070-title">2.5 Full Cell Comparison</h3>
            
            <p>The best hard carbon candidates for LIBs, NIBs, and KIBs (G1000, G1500, and G1000, respectively) were selected to pair against the corresponding cathodes for full cell testing, based on their specific capacity and rate capability (see Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information, for the details of each full cell).</p>
            
            <p>In the Li-ion full cell (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202304091-fig-0005">5a</a>), a commercial LiNi<sub>0. 8</sub>Co<sub>0. 1</sub>Mn<sub>0. 1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NCM811) was selected as the cathode as its sloping capacity (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a>, Supporting Information) complemented that of G1000, allowing fast charging capacity. The G1000 anode was pre-lithiated in a half cell by cycling at 0.1 C for one cycle to form the SEI layer. The Li-ion full cell (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304091-fig-0005">5d</a>) delivered a high discharge capacity of over 380 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 30&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> and 208 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 3 A g<sup>−1</sup> (both the capacity and the current were the weight of active materials in anode). In contrast to the half-cell rate capability, NCM811 had much-improved kinetics and lower overpotential compared with metallic lithium. Such a configuration enabled the Li-ion full cell to achieve a high power density of up to 2263.5&nbsp;W kg<sup>−1</sup> at an energy density of 158.4&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> and a high energy density of up to 321.7&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> at a power density of 21.7&nbsp;W kg<sup>−1</sup> (at coin cell level, the energy density and power density were calculated based on the total weight of active materials in both anode and cathode).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202304091-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/eef41c84-a199-4327-84f1-80ee8c2d6709/adma202304091-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/eef41c84-a199-4327-84f1-80ee8c2d6709/adma202304091-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ecdad443-3349-4630-bc91-026d55e84176/adma202304091-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/eef41c84-a199-4327-84f1-80ee8c2d6709/adma202304091-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202304091-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202304091" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical characterization (full cell) for hard carbons used as anodes in LIBs, NIBs, and KIBs. a–c) Schematic diagrams of rocking-chair working principle upon charging of a Li-ion full cell in a configuration of G1000|1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> LiPF<sub>6</sub> in EC: DMC|LiNi<sub>0. 8</sub>Co<sub>0. 1</sub>Mn<sub>0. 1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (a), a Na-ion full cell in a configuration of G1500|1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in EC: DMC|Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (b), and a K-ion full cell in a configuration of G1000|0.8&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> KPF<sub>6</sub> in EC: DEC|K<sub>2</sub>PTCDA (c). d,e) The varying currents for galvanostatic charge–discharge profiles of LIBs (d) and NIBs (e) were at 30, 60, 150, 300, and 600 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, and 1.5 to 3.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. f) The varying currents for galvanostatic charge–discharge profiles of KIBs were at 25, 50, 125, 250, and 500&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> and 1.25 to 2.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>. All the currents and specific capacities were based on the weight of anode materials only and the mass loading was ≈1.5&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>In NIBs, a promising anode can not only contribute both a long plateau capacity but also a long-sloping region with a high ICE. In this case, G1500 with an ICE of over 86% was selected as the best candidate for the Na-ion full cell. Indeed, in a full cell coupled with a high-power cathode, greatly improved rate capability could be achieved because the polarization induced by the metallic Na was eliminated. In this work, the Na-ion full cell (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304091-fig-0005">5b</a>) was configured G1500|1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in EC: DMC|Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>(NVP). Rhombohedral Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, a typical sodium superionic conductor (NASICON), was selected as the cathode because of its high Na-ion mobility and robust structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304091-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304091-bib-0027_R_d154188689e2065" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Nanoscale">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The full cell delivered a high capacity of 329 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C (30&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>), 288 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 2 C (600&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>), and 260 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10 C (3.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304091-fig-0005">5e</a>). Compared with the half-cell rate capability (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304091-fig-0002">2d</a>), the Na-ion full cell achieved a far higher power density of up to 2172&nbsp;W kg<sup>−1</sup> at an energy density of 181.5&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> and a high energy density of up to 242.0&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> at a power density of 22.1&nbsp;W kg<sup>−1</sup> (based on the weight of both anode and cathode at coin cell level).</p>
            
            <p>In KIBs, due to cost-effectiveness, perylene-3,4,9,10-tetracarboxylic dianhydride (PTCDA) was chosen as the cathode (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304091-fig-0005">5c</a>). PTCDA was pre-potassiated to K<sub>2</sub>PTCDA in order to provide active K<sup>+</sup>. The working voltage range of 1 mV to 3&nbsp;V was selected owing to the rapid potential change between 1.5 and 2.5&nbsp;V of the G1000 anode. The K-ion full cell (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304091-fig-0005">5f</a>) delivered a high capacity of 207.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 25&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> and 102.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 2.5 A g<sup>−1</sup> (the specific capacity and current density were only based on the weight of anode in coin cell level). All these cathode materials were commercially available; the details are in Supporting Information.</p>
            
            <p>Overall, although the K-ion full cell showed a much higher capacity than the half-cell configuration, Li-ion and Na-ion full cells still outperformed. In addition, in full cells, ICE became a crucial parameter because an anode with a low ICE dramatically lowered both the power and energy density because a greater amount of cathode would be required to compensate for initial cycle losses. The ICEs of G1000 in LIB and KIB full cells were 51.3% and 60.3%, respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information), which were unsatisfactory for practical applications. G1500 exhibited an ICE of over 86% in the NIB half cell; G1500||NVP NIB was thus selected as the most promising configuration for testing in a pouch cell.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202304091-sec-0080">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202304091-sec-0080-title">2.6 Na-Ion Pouch Cells</h3>
            
            <p>As a proof-of-concept to demonstrate scale-up potential, single-layer Na-ion pouch cells were fabricated pairing G1500 with NVP as the cathode. In the pouch cell shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202304091-fig-0006">6a</a>, the mass loadings had been further increased (anode: ≈4&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>, cathode: ≈16&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>). Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a>, Supporting Information shows how to pair a G1500 with a NVP.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202304091-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e54b27e8-4ea9-4b7e-977c-9900a13ad8f6/adma202304091-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/e54b27e8-4ea9-4b7e-977c-9900a13ad8f6/adma202304091-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/7aa4d288-dfd9-46cb-b8df-cbd152752b1a/adma202304091-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e54b27e8-4ea9-4b7e-977c-9900a13ad8f6/adma202304091-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202304091-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202304091" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Performance of G1500 anode in practical Na-ion pouch cells. a) Schematic diagram of a Na-ion pouch cell in a configuration of G1500||NVP with a photo of the pouch cell (4.6&nbsp;cm × 5.8&nbsp;cm) inset. b) Galvanostatic charge–discharge of a Na-ion pouch cell at increasing current densities. c) Ragone plot of the G1500||NVP in comparison to other Na-ion full cells, Ref. a,<sup>[</sup><span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#adma202304091-bib-0028" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304091-bib-0028_R_d154188689e2169" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - J. Energy Chem.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Ref. b,<sup>[</sup><span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#adma202304091-bib-0029" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304091-bib-0029_R_d154188689e2174" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Chem. Eng. J.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Ref. c (diglyme electrolyte system),<sup>[</sup><span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#adma202304091-bib-0030" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304091-bib-0030_R_d154188689e2179" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Adv. Mater.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Ref. d,<sup>[</sup><span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#adma202304091-bib-0031" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304091-bib-0031_R_d154188689e2185" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J. Ind. Eng. Chem.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Ref. e,<sup>[</sup><span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#adma202304091-bib-0031" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304091-bib-0031_R_d154188689e2190" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J. Ind. Eng. Chem.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Ref. f,<sup>[</sup><span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#adma202304091-bib-0032" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304091-bib-0032_R_d154188689e2196" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Ref. g.<sup>[</sup><span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#adma202304091-bib-0016" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304091-bib-0016_R_d154188689e2201" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Funct. Mater. ACS Energy Lett.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> d) Ultralong-term cycling performance of a Na-ion pouch cell charged and discharged at the same current density of 300&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>; the specific capacity and currents were all based on the mass of the anode material only. e) The galvanostatic charge–discharge of a three-electrode pouch cell (a separate Na metal electrode as the reference) at 300&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The rate capability of the pouch cell is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304091-fig-0006">6b</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information summarizes the details of the Na-ion pouch cell. Compared to the equivalent coin cell (see Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304091-fig-0005">5e</a>), the rate capability of the pouch cell was slightly decreased because of the high mass loading, which led to reduced conductivity and a longer diffusion pathway for Na-ions. Despite this, the pouch cell demonstrated a more practical scenario, still delivering a high discharge power density of 1783.8&nbsp;W kg<sup>−1</sup> at a discharge energy density of 129.7&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> and a high discharge energy density of 209&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> at a discharge power density of 19.3&nbsp;W kg<sup>−1</sup> (based on the total weight of both anode and cathode active materials), which compares favorably to previous reported NIB full cell systems (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304091-fig-0006">6c</a>). Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information, summarizes the electrochemical performance of G1500 with reported hard carbons in the literature. Taking all components (electrode materials, binders, conductive agents, separator, electrolyte, tabs, current collectors, and Al plastic casing) into account, its discharge power density became 443.3&nbsp;W kg<sup>−1</sup> at a discharge energy density of 32.2&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>; and a discharge energy density of 51.9&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> at a discharge power density of 4.8&nbsp;W kg<sup>−1</sup>. Ultralong-term cycling (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304091-fig-0006">6d</a>) of the pouch cell using ester electrolyte at a current density of 300&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> was conducted, demonstrating high-capacity retention of 88.64% after cycling for over 9000 h (375 days, 5142 cycles) at 256.85 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24</a>, Supporting Information shows another pouch cell in parallel, demonstrating reproducible stability and high retention. A three-electrode pouch cell with a separate Na metal reference was fabricated to monitor the potential change of both electrodes during cycling. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304091-fig-0006">6e</a> records the evolution of both electrodes and the full cell in two successive galvanostatic charge–discharge cycles at 300&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> (based on the mass of the anode only). At the end of sodiation, when the full cell reaches 3.8&nbsp;V, G1500 reaches 2&nbsp;mV, and importantly, no sign of plating can be observed. This result has positive implications on the fast-charging ability of G1500 and further proves the feasibility of long-term cycling of a NIB hard carbon anode. Our results; therefore, offer a promising starting point from which to optimize the <i>N</i>/<i>P</i> ratio, formation protocols, and the ratio of the electrode components, to enhance the energy and power density of G1500||NVP systems.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202304091-sec-0090">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202304091-sec-0090-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>We have employed a series of hard carbons with different structural features to elucidate the different storage behaviors toward Li-, Na-, and K-ions. Compared with Li<sup>+</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup>, the adsorption of Na<sup>+</sup> at higher voltages was followed by pore filling during the low voltage plateau, which is more favorable for electrochemical performance of NIBs.. Subtle structure variations may lead to a different storage mechanism, demonstrating that it is not only individual structural motifs but their overall balance in the material that control how ions are stored. The best anodes for different alkali-ion batteries are selected for assembling full cells with different cathode materials. Hard carbon anodes are typically suitable for NIBs and KIBs for high-power applications and LIBs for high-energy-density purposes. To demonstrate the commercial potential, a Na-ion pouch cell was assembled with a G1500 anode demonstrating an excellent cycling performance and a high discharge power density of 1783.8&nbsp;W kg<sup>−1</sup> at 218.1&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> (pouch cell level, based on the total weight of both active materials of the anode and cathode), a highly promising result for high-performance sustainable materials for beyond Li batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
